# Complete Guide to Power BI for Data Analysts by Microsoft Press

> In this course, learn ways to import data from various sources, as well as how to transform and cleanse data prior to loading it to a report-ready, self-service-enabled data model. Create visualizations in the Power BI Desktop and then secure, share, and collaborate using various features in the Power BI service. Build good habits and develop in your career as a business analyst or data-driven bus

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press) | 7h 14m
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

We'll spend some time talking about Power BI licensing in lesson 1.3. We'll take a look at the Power BI release cycles in 1.4. We'll talk about setting your environment up so you can follow along with the course materials. We'll show you how to explore data source settings so that you can change those as you're setting your environment up to follow the course. And finally, we're going to introduce you to our business scenario. Let us begin.

#### [Understand the data lifecycle](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so before we get going too far with [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]], let's talk a little bit about the data lifecycle. So, on the far left-hand side, we have people that produce data, and they usually have some type of process and system in which to apply that process to, in order to put data into a system. So, if you think about somebody scanning inventory as it's coming in through a loading dock, if you think of a grocery clerk scanning items as they come through out of a person's cart, these are all people that are producing data using some predefined process, and then that data ultimately gets stored in some type of technology, so we have our data, and as those transactions are happening on a daily basis, we then have groups on the other side, which are data consumers, that typically follow some type of process for doing analytics and reporting. So, they will consume how much inventory came in through a backdoor, how much groceries were scanned on the way out the door by the consumers, and how well did we do? What is selling fast, what is selling slow, what are our busy times of day? All these different things happen across this entire data lifecycle. Now, sometimes data producers are also data consumers. So, we have to think about this entire lifecycle
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980&t=101)** whenever we're doing anything in analytics, so other common business processes, aside from inventory, and buying groceries, and things like that, which we just mentioned, let's think of stuff like time sheets, so as people are doing work in a consulting organization, like we have, they're logging their time against certain projects, it's billable or non-billable, and we then use that data for producing invoices for our customers and issuing payroll for our employees, and we really want to make sure that the data across all those processes of a high degree of quality, so it is relevant and reliable, and it comes to us in a timely fashion, so that we can invoice customers on time, and we can manage payroll processes. So, having well-defined business processes that people understand and respect will allow data to come out the other side, so that we can then better analyze our business and manage it over the long haul, and this is a process where a lot of organizations really struggle. Okay, so now, let's talk about a general analytics flow. So, going back to just thinking about the previous slide, if somebody asks a business question, and they say, for example, "Can you tell me the busiest times of day "in my grocery store so I can properly staff?" Then, you would need to go through, and first acquire data from some source, so you'd have to know where that source system is
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980&t=195)** for acquiring the data, you would then have to understand it, and you may inquire with a source system expert, a subject matter expert, to help you understand the data as it is being stored and transacted in that system. In general, most systems don't have the degree of [[Data Quality]] you would like, so you spend a lot of time doubting whether you're getting the right information, whether it's correct, and things like that, and then you go through a process of transformation. So, after you've sorted things out from an understanding and doubt perspective, then we go through and transform our data, and once it's done our transformations, then we perform analysis on it, and then ultimately present that data out to users, and typically, that just generates more questions on the data-consumer side, saying, "Well that's great, we didn't know that, "but what about this now?" So, as you will find out, analytics is a highly-iterative process. So, we like to call these the general stages of answering a business question, so all of the work from front to back that happens, to ask and answer a business question, and I would like to point out that the first four items there: acquire, understand, doubt, and transform, one thing that we really emphasize with Power BI is that you will hear me say many times through this course, "With Power BI, when done properly, "we can do a whole lot more with a whole lot less." So, one of the really nice things about Power BI
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980&t=291)** is the ability to build data sets, and then ultimately share those data sets out, which is typically really challenging inside of an [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] environment. We find Excel environments typically have lots of spreadsheets that overlap each other, so they're very similar, if someone else wants a little bit more information, if somebody wants it in a slightly different spin, and you end up with many, many spreadsheets doing very similar things. In Power BI, we spend a lot of work in those first four areas, to make sure that we build data sets that are trustable, timely, and ultimately shareable, which really reduces the amount of data sprawl in an organization, and gives you a better shot towards achieving a central version of the truth. Now, it's not easy by any means. It takes lots of teamwork, lots of coordination, but the facilities are there inside of Power BI to really allow for that, and then, when we move to the far right-hand side there, we can start using those data sets over and over again to analyze, and present, and make the overall analytics process go much faster over the course of time. And we will see as we move through the course, you have the ability to create fewer reports and put security on top of those reports, to allow users to all access a single report at a high level, but only see the data that they're allowed to. So, when used properly, Power BI can really reduce the amount of artifacts that you have in your environment, and typically, it's the number of artifacts and the overlap
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-data-lifecycle?u=76281980&t=386)** which really gets people into lots of trouble from a central-version-of-the-truth perspective, because trying to keep them all in sync, and updating them, and making sure they're all answering the same business questions consistently is often an impossible challenge. So, walking down this path and thinking about this at a higher level is a much better way to manage your organization. All right, so just a little bit of food for thought, and with that, we're at the end of the sub lesson.

#### [Understand the Power BI environment](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so let's spend a few minutes talking about the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] high-level architecture. And it's really important just to kind of step back and think about what all the pieces of Power BI are and what significance they play and how they fit together. We find this really gives users a good firm understanding in terms of what is happening, because when you're new to any technology, there's all kinds of terms and buzzwords and things being misused. It's really hard to paint a picture in your head how things work. So let's go through this. So we have a dotted line that runs down the middle of the screen there. And what is below that line is generally what we like to say on-premise and above is what we refer to as in the cloud. So, what we're going to work a lot with in this course is the Power BI Desktop. And what we're going to do with the Power BI Desktop is we're going to go ahead and consume data from our different sources. So we're going to bring data from cloud sources and on-premise sources. In this particular course here, we give you a bunch of [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] spreadsheets and text files, which generally get put somewhere on your machine. And those would then be on-premise sources. Perhaps when you take these files and download them, you'll put them in a [[Microsoft OneDrive|OneDrive]] or some other source and then those are actually tactically sitting in the cloud. But there's no point splitting hairs in this course here. You're going to get some Excel spreadsheets and text files, but for the most part you're going to find those being stored on-premise, but it's really up to you where you ultimately put them. And we're going to go ahead
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980&t=101)** and do lots of work in the Power BI desktop with that data and build some models, build some reports, and then we're going to publish it into the Power BI Service. So once it's in the Power BI Service, that's where we can access our reporting and datasets through a web browser. And when it's ultimately published up into the Power BI Service, it within seconds is available on the Power BI mobile device if you have it enabled in your organization. So we do all this work in the Power BI Desktop to build data models, build some reporting on top of it, secure it, then we go ahead and publish that into the Power BI Service. And this is where people are going to consume that information through. Okay and once we do that, then there's some further steps we can do. You would then want to think about, all right, well if my data's sitting on premise, how do I get my data updated in the power based service on a periodic basis? Now, the most classic way to update data sources is overnight. For the most part, most warehousing systems that I've been part of through my entire career do some type of overnight update at an off peak time, pulling data out of the source systems, doing the transformations, and then building the reporting after the fact. And that data gateway allows for a secure connection point between your on-premise sources and the cloud so that whenever Power BI wants to refresh itself up in the service based on the schedule you set, it has a way to go down and get the data from those source systems that are on-premise.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980&t=194)** Additionally, you can go ahead and consume data out of cloud sources directly to the Power BI Service as well. So let's go ahead and take a look at the Power BI end-to-end process flow. So this effectively is how our course is going to flow. So we start on the far left-hand side by acquiring our data sources. So if you recall back to the previous sub lesson, we talked about the general analytics process flow. And it mirrors this in a lot of ways. So we are going to go in and find this data source that we need to answer the business questions that are being asked of us. We will then spend a lot of time working inside the [[Microsoft Power Query|power query]] to transform and cleanse data. And once we're done that we want need to load the data somewhere. So we take that data and we load it into the data model. Once it's in the data model, we're going to build relationships, we're going to do some formatting on the model, build some hierarchies, and then put some [[DAX]] expressions and work with DAX formulas in our model. Once we've done those first few steps there, I like to use the term report ready. So we've done a lot of work to make sure that we've got data that isn't a report ready state. Once it's there, then we're going to go ahead to the report canvas and build visualizations and consider how do we make those visuals compelling and flow together to tell a story that the business users are asking. Once we're done all that work on the Power BI Desktop, so you see the line goes all the way across the top and ends on the publish process, and that typically all happens in the Power BI Desktop.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980&t=288)** Then we want to take that work and publish it into a workspace. In particular my workspace. And then from there we're going to consume that information, share it out to others, ensure we've got the security put on top of it, and then take steps to govern that work. And I have to admit from my personal perspective, the governance side of Power BI is the most exciting side of Power BI. But at this point in time, probably the least tapped side of Power BI. And the reason I say that is for many years in my career, organizations have been striving for a central version of the truth and trying to get there and struggling with it for many reasons. But Power BI now has all the capabilities from a technical perspective to support you getting there. Now the big challenge here right now is exposing organizations to what those features and functions actually are, and then utilizing that data to make your analyst environment that much more efficient. So the data's all there, the processes are all there. It's more now a matter of educating people of what it is all about, what it's useful for, and then starting to use that to drive efficiencies in your organization. Now, I think into 2024 and beyond, governance is going to be a giant topic we're going to see. A lot of organizations spending a lot of time on. Like say, that's something that I'm most excited about. Don't get me wrong, I certainly love the [[Data Acquisition]] and the modeling side of things and building reports, but the biggest challenge has been how do we manage and govern that so that people are using what we build and we can continuously iterate on a coordinated fashion
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-the-power-bi-environment?u=76281980&t=382)** with that stuff and get the users and organization to start using that more and more and more, and bring things into the realm of the central version of the truth. So the puzzle pieces there, it's the people and process now that really needs to catch up. So with that, we're at the end of the sub lesson.

#### [Understand Power BI licensing](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-power-bi-licensing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-power-bi-licensing?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so let's spend a few minutes talking about individual licensing, 'cause licensing can always be a bit of a fun topic in any piece of software. So the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] desktop itself, which we're going to spend a lot of time with in this course is a free download. Anybody can go and download it, install it in their machine and use it. So you can bring data in, clean it up, build reporting, and essentially at that point you have something that really looks an awful lot like what you would be doing inside of [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. The Power BI service, your license as an individual is either free or pro. So you can have a free Power BI service license, and that has some pros and cons, which we'll talk about a little bit. Or you have a pro license. So pretty simple so far. So then from the Power BI service perspective, your workspace, which you're going to publish your work, is either in premium capacity or non-premium capacity. So one of those two. So let's just go and recap here once again, the Power BI high level architecture. So once again, if you keep in mind we're going to work inside the Power BI desktop right there, which is our free download. And you can go ahead and consume sources, go ahead and build reports and publish your models into some part of the Power BI service. Now, once it's in the Power BI service, this is ultimately where you're going to endeavor to share your work. But if you have a free license, depending on the workspace you're publishing into, your users may or may not be able to see that. So we'll have a grid here in a few minutes,
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-power-bi-licensing?u=76281980&t=99)** which is going to talk about that. And then once again, once it's in the Power BI service, it's available on the mobile device. Then we have the gateway in place and you can consume more data from your cloud sources. So let's just take a look at this from a licensing perspective. So Power BI desktop, free download. Power BI service, we need to consider the workspace we're publishing our work to and the different types of licenses. So once again, pulling data, publishing it up. And then we need to consider a few things. So the grid goes something like this. And let's first consider publishing your work into non-premium capacity, which is where a lot of people are going to publish their work. I would say the majority of work is published into non-premium capacity. So let's start there. So if it's in non-premium capacity and you're a free user, you can use this as a personal sandbox where you create content for yourself and interact with that content. A free license is a great way to try out the Power BI service. You can't consume content from anyone else or share your content with others. So it is essentially a box where you can do your work as an individual. And that kind of defeats in some ways the real power of Power BI is sharing your out to others through the web browser or the mobile device. But at the same time, you may be on a path right now where you're just trying to discover what Power BI is all about and you get comfortable with yourself. And if that's the case, publishing your work in a non-premium capacity using a free license is a great way to do that. So let's consider if you're not premium capacity
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-power-bi-licensing?u=76281980&t=193)** and you have a pro license. So in this case you can collaborate with premium per user and pro users by creating and sharing content. So you can then share your work with other users that have a pro license or users that are premium per user. Alright, simple enough so far. So let's consider premium capacity. So what if an organization has premium capacity? So the users may have a free license in the service and that then allows you to interact with the content assigned to you through your premium capacity and that is shared with you. Free premium per user and pro users can collaborate without requiring the free users to have a pro account. So if the work is published in a premium capacity, then you as a free user of Power BI in the service can get at that content. And then finally, considering the premium capacity of a pro license, you can collaborate with free, premium per user and pro users by creating and sharing content. So that seems simple enough on the grid, let's take a look at it here in a visualization. This often helps clarify things. So we have on the left hand side a user with a pro license. And they take their work and they publish it into shared capacity. So this is non-premium capacity. So at that point in time, if you publish it into workspace A, the only people that can actually see what you've published, if you share it with them, are users that have a pro license. If you take that same work as a pro user
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-power-bi-licensing?u=76281980&t=287)** and publish it into premium dedicated capacity, then users with a pro license or the free license can consume your work. So this is just a consideration you need to make when you're getting into Power BI. If you have sufficient users, premium capacity starts to become something that is attractive from a costing perspective. If you have few users, then the shared capacity is generally the preferred way to go, unless there's some features inside of premium that you really want to want to get at. But that is well beyond the scope of this level one course. So for now, just consider the fact that there's free licenses and pro licenses, and you have shared capacity and premium capacity, and just understanding a high level how all of that works from a licensing perspective. Alright, so I made it as simple as I could. And with that, we're at the end of the sub-lesson.

>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/introduce-our-business-scenario?u=76281980&t=194)** is the first stop that we see people working on in Power BI is automating an existing process. And just by taking something like sales that AdventureWorks was receiving two times a month and giving it to them more frequently, that is often a huge win. Now, when it comes to how Power BI is sold, everything is sold through the visualizations and the interactions, and things like that. And I understand that those are hugely important pieces to Power BI, but sometimes the users are fine with, "I like what I get for reporting, but just give it to me more frequently so I can make better decisions about my business." And so we often see that that is the first scenario that people work on, is taking an onerous process that can only be run twice a month, because it's a four to five hour process and who wants to do that every day, take a look at how to automate that process so you can run it on a nightly basis, unattended, and have that reporting delivered out to the users on a morning-by-morning basis, with security put on top of it, with zero manual intervention. So that is the guiding star inside of Power BI, is to getting to that type of world. So that's often one of the things we see users doing after they take this course, is going and automating an existing process so that they can deliver with more frequency and more confidence. And you're going to hear me talking a lot through these courses, Power BI allows you to do more with a whole lot less. So you'll have fewer data models in Power BI versus Excel. You'll have fewer reports in Power BI versus Excel.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/introduce-our-business-scenario?u=76281980&t=288)** And a lot of this is driven through the architecture inside of Power BI, with the separation of data and reporting, and then the ability to put security on top of your data model, which is very difficult to do inside of Excel and really causes users to reinvent the wheel over and over and over again, which then leads to maintenance problems, conflicting information, and a divergence from the central truth. If you do things right with Power BI, you can converge on the central truth, but that does take some time. The tooling is there for it. So let's just step back. And luckily enough, AdventureWorks is a very good culture and the organization just wants to do better, so you have everybody in behind you, helping you to get to that daily reporting standpoint. ### 2. Importing and Transforming Data Using Power Query Editor

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives?u=76281980&t=6)** - As data analysts, chances are you have spent or will spend a lot of time wrangling data that comes in many shapes and sizes and from a multitude of disparate sources. In most organizations, data is scattered and sometimes not managed properly, which complicates the process of preparing data for analytics. [[Data Preparation]] is often a data analyst's least favorite task. It is estimated that 70% of the work in any analytic endeavor is spent getting your data report ready. The task of consuming and transforming data has long been one of the hardest, yet most important skills for a data analyst to master as they prepare datasets for consumption and presentation. This usually involves removing rows and columns, cleaning erroneous column values, enhancing datasets with new columns, and merging the pending datasets. If you've used [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] for analytics, as many you have for years, you've relied on the mastery of advanced functions inside of Excel, such as VLOOKUP, IF, FIND, CLEAN, and SUBSTITUTE, on top of many traditional import means. In this lesson, we'll explore the capabilities of PowerGrid to help simplify and better automate the process of ingesting and transforming data. If you're used to traditional means using Excel, this functionality is often considered a game changer. Some of the key features of PowerGrid are the following. It has many data connectors available and more being added periodically. You'll see the data gathering and shaping capabilities are fast, easy, and intuitive to use. And one of my favorite things are that transformations are recorded, which allows you to repeat the steps to save manual and repetitive work. Plus, there's some excellent documentation features we will cover. So what does all this add up to?
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives?u=76281980&t=99)** It is often estimated that optimizing the repetitive activities around collecting, combining, and transforming data using [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]] will cut your data preparation time by up to 80%. What we're going to do here is we're going to look at, in Power Query, connecting to data sources, bringing them through the Power Query Editor, doing the extractions, transforming and cleansing data, taking a look at the M language, which is all transformations, and we'll look at loading data into our data model. The learning objectives here that we're going to do an overview of Power Query in 2.1. In 2.2, we'll import United States sales data. We'll import customer data. In 2.4, we'll import sales territory data. We'll import product data in 2.5. Product roll-up in 2.6. Then look at merging the product and product query roll-up queries together. We'll import the other countries' sales data, append the United States sales or other countries' sales query. We'll create columns, We'll manage model loading and cleanup. And finally, we'll organize and document Power Query. Let us begin.

So what we kind of like to say is the old habits of analytics are to bring everything in just in case. And that's not a real good way to build a model in any environment. Power BI being just one of many different analytics environments that users can use. So bring the data in as you need it. So it's a very important rule for you to follow. And like I said, traditionally I find that a lot of people in organizations are just used to bringing everything in just in case you happen to need it. So that just ends up over-inflating models, making them not that performant and difficult to use. And then users ultimately start using all the columns you give. And then what happens when you want to go ahead and get rid of one of those 'cause you find there's something wrong with it, you've usually got a very large ball of spaghetti to untangle, which generally isn't a fun situation to be in. > **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/import-product-data?u=76281980&t=196)** So as I add new rows to Excel table, from an Excel perspective, it will pull the data only from that table and not the data surrounding it. So had I been pulling this from the sheet all of a sudden now this piece of data here that wasn't there a moment ago would be significant and impact things. So that's just one thing to be aware of. Excel tables are wonderful to connect into, they give you structure inside of Excel. But I would hazard to say that the overall majority of Excel users don't take advantage of Excel tables. So as much as they're really nice for structuring data, you don't see a lot of Excel users using them. If you're an organization that has a heavy use fixed cell tables and they're do being used properly, that's fantastic 'cause that is not something we see happening across a lot of organizations. So I'm just going to go ahead and remove that and let's get ourselves back to the Power BI desktop. All right, so now what we're going to do is we are going to go ahead and get rid of some columns. So what we're going to do this time, instead of getting rid of specific columns, we're going to tell Power Query which columns we actually want to keep. So we're going to do the exact opposite and this is actually a preferred way of removing columns from our dataset. So when I go ahead and do that, now we're going to see that the unit of measure was the one that was removed, and we're keeping these columns. The reason this is a preferred way of doing things is this is explicitly saying these are the columns that are important to me versus the ones you want to get rid of. And why that is important is let's just hypothetically say that all of a sudden somebody put some sensitive information into one of the data files you have. If they put that sensitive information in there and it wasn't part of your removed column's functionality, then that column would flow automatically through into your dataset and that may not be something you want to have happen. #### [Merge the product and product rollup queries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/merge-the-product-and-product-rollup-queries?u=76281980)

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/merge-the-product-and-product-rollup-queries?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so what we are going to do next, given that we just imported the product rollup query and we previously created the product query, what we're going to do now is merge these two queries together. And the reason we want to do that is we want to take the product category from the product query, which we just brought in. And we want this column's values to show up over in this query as an additional column. So, we would like to do that for a couple of reasons. Number one, is that when it comes to end user self-service, we want to make sure that we have the fewest number of tables possible. Generally, that just makes models less intimidating. The fewer the tables, the fewer columns, the more approachable your model is. That's one reason. The second reason is we're going to do this for performance benefits. We're going to essentially later on prevent the product rollup table from showing up in the data model, and we're only going to bring the one column that we care about. So, we're going to do it for that. And the third reason, which is perhaps one of the more important ones, is that later on we're going to go ahead and build a hierarchy on our product information that goes from individual physical [[Microsoft Products|products]] to product subcategories and then to categories. And one of the rules on hierarchies that all columns of participate in the hierarchy need to exist in the same table. So, let's go ahead and merge these two queries together so that we have all the information sitting in one table
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/merge-the-product-and-product-rollup-queries?u=76281980&t=102)** for the benefits that we just mentioned. If I uncheck this and it runs into a file that does not match the first file, the entire load simply fails. And believe it or not, of the two options, I consider that the preferable of the two options as a level one user. Just simply let it load fail, come in, see what happened, correct it, and then rerun your load and refresh and then let the data come through. I find that it's better just to not have the data there at all than give people partial data and they start making decisions based on that partial data. And you'll notice the time portion of the date is chopped off. A best practice inside of Power Query is to go ahead and separate date and time into two individual columns if you need both. Now, it's very rare that you don't need the actual date component of a column, but a little less frequent that you actually need the time of day. So in this case here, the time of day did show up, but was not something I needed for this analysis so I just simply got rid of it. But if I did need that time of day, I would go through and add it as an extra column and make it a time dimension, which is going to allow us to do all kinds of different analysis on dates and times separately, which is much more flexible. So as the best practice inside of Power Query, we want to go ahead and make sure that each column only contains the portion that is relevant to it. So in this case, the order date wants to be the date. And if time was important, I would say order date time and create that as a separate column with the time component alone. This model does not need that. Okay, so just going to go through and take a look at the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of my data types. So I'm happy with whole number there. Column A and B are the same across both queries, but query 1 has column C and query 2 has column X, so there's a difference in columns. So Power Query once again is friendly, it will allow the append to happen. The columns that match will be matched up and those to do not will just be brought into the final data set. And the row values for the columns, that didn't exist in the initial query, just get left as null. So these values right here in X that have the rows from query number 1, will just be null.

### 3. Enhancing the Data Model: The Basics

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives-20270073?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives-20270073?u=76281980&t=6)** - In this lesson, we're going to build a data model to support self-service reporting. And the data model is the center of the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] universe. So with that, we're going to go through this slowly 'cause it is different from what you may be used to doing. But all the hard work you're going to do will pay off. So what we're going to do here is we're going to try and balance simplicity for end users, model size for performance, and we want to make our models easy to maintain. So in this lesson, we're going to talk about navigating the Power BI desktop. We're going to understand what the dimensional model is and why you want to use it. We're going to create relationships and manage settings, modify column properties. We're going to learn how to create column groups. We'll talk about creating hierarchies. We'll learn to optimize our relationships, and we're going to do some model cleanup. Let us begin.

#### [Understanding Dimensional Modeling](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understanding-dimensional-modeling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understanding-dimensional-modeling?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] All right. So Dimensional Modeling is a [[Data Modeling]] methodology that centers around measuring a business process. The business processes are known as facts, and when we measure business processes, we need ways to describe those measurements. And for this, we will use dimensions as report labels and or filters. The combination of facts and dimensions is known as a Star Schema or a Dimensional Model. The business process that we are working on in this class is a sales model. So as you can imagine, you have an operational system out there that is collecting sales. So as customers purchase items from you, those transactions are being recorded individually. And as those transactions are being recorded, descriptions of those sales amounts are occurring as well. So a certain customer bought that, they were in a certain sales territory, a certain product was bought, and they were bought on a given date. So the transaction happens, and there's a bunch of different ways that we go ahead and describe those. Essentially, those will amount to our dimensions. So in our model, we're going to take the sales transactions, which is the business process, and we're going to put those measurements inside of our fact table. So we will build a table called sales, which will be the fact table in this dimensional model. We will then create several dimensions, and this is only a few, as we will build
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understanding-dimensional-modeling?u=76281980&t=99)** a territory dimension, a product dimension, later on, we will build a date dimension, we also have a custom dimension in our model as well. If the model is set up like this, such that there is a fact table in the middle and only one dimension table on the outside, this is referred to as a Star Schema. So for example, back in our product dimension, if you recall, back in [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]], we took the product query and combined it with the product roll-up query, so that we had all the information for creating a hierarchy all within one table. Now, we went through and did that merge, but if we did not and we left our dimension in two separate tables, something like this, we would then ultimately end up building a relationship between the product category and the product and we would have a second table out from the fact table and this is referred to as a [[Snowflake]] Schema. As a level one user of [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]], I recommend sticking with the Star Schema modeling. Once you start doing more with Power BI and get into levels two and beyond, there are some advantages to the Snowflake Schema, but for now, let's keep things nice and simple and follow the Star Schema modeling. So what we had done before, by combining the Dim product category and Dim product together into one table, we're going to take that approach. So what are some of the benefits of a dimensional model? So essentially it makes your [[Data Structures]]
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understanding-dimensional-modeling?u=76281980&t=194)** easier to navigate, manage, and consume over the course of time. It is easier to layer in more information while keeping the model results predictable. It enables consistent analysis across an organization. So if members of your organization can agree on different dimensionality and hierarchies and how they want to pull data together, we can start achieving more consistent analysis across the organization. And at the end of the day, Power BI itself is optimized to consume this type of model. So for those of you that have never heard of a dimensional model or done it, it's actually not that difficult. In fact, I would argue it's simpler than doing any modeling you've likely done in the [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] world but the biggest challenge of a dimensional model is the fact that it's different. If you come from the Excel world, you're used to taking all your data and bringing it into one table so that you can eventually put a pivot table on top of it so that you can start building all kinds of reporting on top of that. So there's this notion of pulling all your data into one giant table. We in the Power BI world refer to that as the one big table model, but in Power BI, we actually leave our tables alone in the structures that we just defined here and at the end of the day, after you get used to this, it is a whole lot easier and a whole lot quicker to go ahead and build your model. Like I mentioned before, the biggest hurdle in this is the fact that it is different. So what we will do is show you how to build your data model up and store information in the model
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understanding-dimensional-modeling?u=76281980&t=289)** so that it's more easy to consume and we'll use all of these terms over the next few lessons so that you become familiar with how this is all pulled together. Alright, so with that, we're at the end of the sub lesson.

>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-relationships-and-manage-settings?u=76281980&t=380)** what you're doing in Power BI in order to take advantage of this relationship and use it effectively. And as a level one user of Power BI, you do not have the ability to use many-to-many relationships properly. So we want to avoid many-to-many relationships at all costs as a level one user and we can move away from this very easily. So, what I'm going to do is just click okay. I'm going to accept this relationship and I can see it gets built. I'll click close, I get the line drawn between sales and product on the product key. And now this time I see a star on both sides, which is indicating many-to-many and my filters can flow in both directions. So the easiest way to fix this is going back into [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]] and de-duplicating our product table. So, what essentially has happened is that we have duplicate [[Microsoft Products|products]] in this table right here, which is creating that many-to-many state. And often the way to get away from a many-to-many relationship is simply just to deduplicate your dimension tables. So I'm going to go back into Power Query. We're going to click transform data. I am going to go find my product dimension right here and we'll let it get pulled up. And what we're going to see here is if I just take a look at my first four rows, these are actually duplicates. I'll need one instance of product key 210 and if I take a look across all the column values here, they are the same values across all columns for those four rows. So all I simply have to do is ask Power Query to deduplicate this product table.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-relationships-and-manage-settings?u=76281980&t=475)** The way I'm going to do it is by clicking product key, going to the far right hand side, hold the shift key down, click on product category. Now what I'm going to do is I'm going to deduplicate across all of these columns. So any two rows that have the same value across all columns will be considered a duplicate and Power Query will only keep one. So, what I can do is go to the home tab. So I'm going to go through and de-duplicate this, go back to my remove rows right here and say remove duplicates. So what I'd actually to do here is I want to go back into Power Query and I want to actually standardize these column names first before I go ahead and build this. So this is something that I should have actually done as part of Power Query, but perhaps I forgot. So I'm just going to click cancel, click close. I'm now going to go back over into Power Query and what I'm going to do here is go to my sales territory table and I'm just going to rename each of these columns here. I'm going to call region sales territory region and I'm going to go into the country and I am going to rename it to sales territory country. So I've gone through and done my renaming of standardization. I'm going to click close and apply again 'cause I want to reapply all the data to my model. And now we'll see here that we've got sales territory country, sales territory key and sales territory region. So now instead of going back to manage relationships, I actually want to show you a quicker way to do this.
>
> **[11:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-relationships-and-manage-settings?u=76281980&t=662)** So, what I can do here is I'm going to click on sales territory region in the sales table and then hold my mouse key down my left mouse key and drag it over top of sales territory region over in the sales territory table. If I do that, now I can see that I get my relationship built up and it is a one-to-many relationship with filters flowing from my dimension table down to my fact table. And as we take a look at our model, we want ideally our filters to flow from the dimensions down to the fact tables. Later on what we're going to do is we're going to make it so the only items that'll be visible for report writers. In the report view are going to be our metrics. So the things we're trying to sum average, min, max and count and effectively that's going to do is prevent people from ever choosing values in the fact table for doing filtering. We're actually going to force people to have to use the dimension tables for doing our filters. Okay, so let's just go ahead and bring up some animations here to describe cardinality a little bit more. So first, the definition of cardinality. So cardinality is official non-database dictionary definition is mathematical. It is the number of values in a set, but when applied to [[Databases]], the meaning is a bit different. It's the number of distinct values in a table column relative to the number of rows in the table. Repeated values in the column do not count. So, what relationship direction means, it means whatever direction the relationship is
>
> **[12:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-relationships-and-manage-settings?u=76281980&t=756)** that determines how Power BI filters the data. So let's take a look at cardinality here. So keep in mind, prior to our deduplication of the product dimension, we had something that looks like this. So we had our product table having some duplicates in it. So if you notice here, Road Bike shows up multiple times and the relationship will look something like this. So Road Bike in the product dimension shows up three times in this example and two times over in the sales transaction. So that is our many-to-many. Mountain Bike, same thing. So twice in product, three times over in sales transactions. So after doing deduplication, which is what we did back in Power Query, we reduced the product dimension down to unique rows. So when we go ahead and do that, we now get into a one-to-many state. So one Road Bike matches three over the sales transactions. Mountain Bike two and Jersey matches like so. And for sake of completeness, we should go ahead and describe the one-to-one cardinality. Although it's not that often used, it's where we have one row relating only to one row in the other table and that is it. Alright, so with that, we are at the end of this lesson.

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-dax?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Presenter] So, first off, what is [[DAX]]? DAX stands for Data Analysis Expressions. It was modeled initially after the [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] formula language, it is a functional language, much like Excel, so if you're an Excel user and you've written Excel expressions, you're used to nesting functions inside of functions and, therefore, using the DAX language will have a certain degree of familiarity to you. It is used to encapsulate business logic in your data model, and some examples of the use of DAX are calculating a full name from a first name and last name, calculating a gross margin percentage, or perhaps even calculating a year-over-year percentage change. And back many years ago when I first started to teach DAX, I had somebody ask me a very interesting question. They said, "I've been using [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] for a number of years now, I've built lots of reporting, my executives really love it. They get to interact with their data, it's timely, it's automated, and I've never had to write a stitch of DAX. Why do I need to learn it?" And it stumped me for a moment and then I sat back and thought, "Are you just doing basic aggregations?" And the answer was yes. So if you recall, back when we were in our [[Data Modeling]] section talking about the basics, we went through and created default summarizations on our numeric columns. And by virtue of that, when somebody goes to use those columns, there is by default a summarization there and the Power BI engine will automatically write
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-dax?u=76281980&t=100)** implicit DAX for you. So if I take my line total sales and it had a default summarization of sum, and I take it and drag it on my canvas, Power BI will automatically write a SUM DAX function for you, even though you don't need to do it. All you did was provide the default summarization. So that's in fact what this user did, they basically were just doing all the reporting off of basic sums, average min-max account, so our general basic aggregations, and they never had to write DAX 'cause they took advantage of the default summarizations. But chances are, when you start doing more with Power BI you're going to want to move beyond basic aggregations, and you're also going to want the ability to reuse those calculations in more complex calculations. So one of the challenges with default summarizations, as we built them earlier, is you can only use them once in the basic mode. You have no ability to go ahead and reuse them, so we're going to show you a bunch of examples as to why creating DAX Expressions does become a much more preferred way of doing things over and above the default summarizations, and we're going to show you how to build basic DAX Expressions out and then use them in more complex calculations. So the first part of the expression we see here on the screen is the name of the column or measure that we're going to go ahead and create. So in this case here we're creating a column called full name, and the way we're going to calculate that
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-dax?u=76281980&t=195)** is by taking the first name from the customer table and the last name from the customer table, and we're going to put a space between the two so it's nicely formatted. So the name can contain spaces, so the full name can and should contain a space, we want to make it English readable and understandable. It's followed by the equal sign, and this returns the value to the right side of the equal sign. So as this full name is being calculated it's returned into the row value for full name. So as I mentioned a moment ago, we are creating a calculated column which is concatenating the first name and last name from the customer table, and the reference to customer square bracket first name is referred to as the fully qualified name. So when we are creating this we technically do not need the table name in front of the column name, as long as the table we're creating the expression in is considered the current table. So we'll show you an example of that in a moment, but it's preferred to actually keep the table name in front of the column name for a few reasons. It makes the code a whole lot more readable, so I can instantaneously look at this and say, "I know where that first name is coming from." So it's quicker for you to read, as a user know where things are coming from and it's actually quicker for the engine as well, 'cause the engine can avoid asking the question that you just would've asked yourself as to where does that column come from? If we make it explicit by putting the table in front, the engine knows exactly where to go get that and immediately grabs it and it saves a processing step. So there are some performance benefits to it.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/understand-dax?u=76281980&t=290)** The table names are required whenever the column is from a different table than the current table, so we just mentioned that, and table names must be unique within the database. So in this particular data model we can only have one table called customer, we cannot have multiple tables called customer we're only allowed to have one. Table names must be enclosed in single quotation marks if they contain spaces, other special characters, or any non-English alphanumeric characters. #### [Create basic DAX expressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Tutor] So, let's just first discuss calculated columns. So as we saw in the previous sub lesson, we went through and built a new column called Full Name and its values were calculated on a row by row basis to create a physical column. When we do that, we build something that takes up space in the data model. So we just need to consider that. And its value is calculated data load time. So as you're refreshing your data model and we're pulling data from sources, running it through [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]] and ultimately landing at the data model, that work happens at load time. Which is generally an off peak time in most organizations. Typically calculated columns reside in dimension tables to be used as labels on reports and or slicers. And often they can be pushed back into the Power Query engine. So for example, back in an earlier sub lesson, we actually built this column back in Power Query. We took the unit price and the order quantity from our sales table and created a brand new column called Line Total Sales, if you recall, we use the multiplication function back in Power Query to create this physical product back in Power Query. And there's often a benefit to doing it back in Power Query, because there's a potential that if you do this back in Power Query, that these calculations can be pushed back to the source system so it can do the heavy lifting for the calculation itself. So, sometimes there's benefits, sometimes there's not. So often we find it's better just to push this back into Power Query, 'cause it centralizes the logic
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=99)** and builds these columns out in a central place and potentially can take advantage of the horsepower of your source systems. Alright, so, measures, we created one of these back over in the previous sub lesson. It is an aggregate of a column. It does not take up space in the model, although it does take up very marginal space because you're actually visibly typing it out and that has to get stored somewhere, but it's negligible. Its value is calculated at runtime. So this has a huge pro and sometimes a little bit of a con as well. So the value is calculated at runtime. This is what gives [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] a ton of its flexibility from the end user's perspective. So they can go ahead and build reports and slice and dice measurements how they see fit on their own. So without having to rely back on IT to build them new reports and give you new aggregations and deliver data sets, you can do all of this on your own. Now the drawback is that this does happen at runtime. So if you, you're building your measures off a poorly formed model, so something that is not star schema based and something that has maybe too much data in it, these measures can start to run quite slowly. And that happens then that you end up with frustrated users and you need to rethink your model. So this is where we really like to tie back to the dimensional modeling technique and say that if you can build your model out using dimensional modeling techniques and from the ground up keep your model nice and simple, so only bringing the columns in you need, only bring the rows in you need, come up with good naming conventions, and respect the dimensional modeling format,
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=193)** you're in general are going to have [[DAX]] expressions that are very simple, and when they're very simple they tend to run a whole lot better. So we'll talk a little bit more about that as we go through this course. Measures themselves, typically reside inside of the fact table. So when we build a measure we often will put it inside of the fact table, although technically the measure itself belongs to the data model. So I could go ahead and move my measure to any table and it will not affect its calculation, because it actually belongs to the data model itself. And we're putting it into a particular table just for logical organization purposes. So we'll do a quick example of that as well. The row context, we will go ahead and show a demo on that here momentarily. But the definition of row context is, the context that is applied as a virtual pointer which moves down a table of data, row by row, and is used in a calculation. So pulling this example back up, I'm looking at my customer table where we initially built our Full Name calculated column. I can see here I got my column called Full Name. And essentially what's happening here is you can think of my mouse pointer as a pointer, which says go to row one and grab the first name and last name and calculate this value. Once it's done, move to row two, do the same thing, row three, four, all the way down to the 18484th row, it we'll go through on a row by row basis to perform that calculation. Filter context on the other hand is the context that is applied by taking all the filters that surround a DAX expression
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=284)** and then applying them to the filter to affect its result. So let's go ahead and take a look first a couple other ways we can affect filter context. Then we'll go through and do a demo. So some other areas where context be applied or affected, are within the visual which we've already seen. The visual level, the page level, the report level, which is considered the all pages level inside of a Power BI report. We can put filters inside of an expression, and later on in the visualization section we'll show you where you can manipulate filter context using visual interactions. But that last point will save for the reporting section. Alright, so let's go ahead and do a demo here. So I am here back on my Power BI desktop, and we can see that I've got page one which has a single visual on it. What I'm going to do here is I'm just going to right click on it and say duplicate page. I want to create a second page here, and I'm just going to call this page two just to make sure that we've got nice naming conventions here. And on page number two, what I'm going to do is I'm actually going to make two copies of this visual. So just to recap here, I've got page number one, which has a single visual on it. So if I go to page one, I can see there's one visual. Page two has two visuals on it. Okay, so what I'm going to do here now is I'm going to go over to my filter context and I can apply filters that will affect all my pages. So all the pages in this report. I can apply filters that affect only a single page. And if I had a visual selected, I can apply filters that only affect a single visual.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=377)** So let's go ahead and give you a quick visualization around this. Alright, so before we dive into the filter context, let's go ahead and discuss the context of the PBX file. So so far we've created a file called "Sales.pbix" and we've saved that, and I've shown you where that exists on our directories. So inside of the PBX file, as I mentioned earlier, is a data set and a report. And inside of the report we can have multiple pages. And on each page we can have zero or many visuals. So when you are going ahead and building your initial PBX file, to you it's just one big file and that's it. So much like [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. But the secret sauce inside of Power BI, in my opinion, is the fact that when you're building this sales PBX file up, or any PX file for that matter, is the separation of the data set and the report. This is huge architecturally when it comes to why Power BI can do so much more with a whole lot less. So that data set becomes one part of the PBX file and the report becomes another. Later on when we go to publish this work into the Power BI service, we're going to see this actually gets broken apart on publish. So that is a topic for a little bit later on, but let's focus more on the report. So inside your PBX file you have a single report. You can only have one report inside of a PBX file. And that report will have the same name as the PBX file.
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-basic-dax-expressions?u=76281980&t=472)** So the report is actually called sales, even though we don't see it here. Inside a report, you have one or many pages, and you have to have a minimum of one page in a report, or you can have up to as many pages as you want. So from a filtering point of view, which is what the brought us to this slide in the first place, is that we can apply filters that are at the report level, or inside the filters pane you'll see this called filters on all pages. Or we can apply a filter at the page level, which will affect only a single page, or we can apply a filter at a visual level, which will affect a single visual. So there's different levels in which we can apply filters and that will influence different things based on the level that we're applying that filter. #### [Use the ALL function](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-all-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-all-function?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] All right, so in this sub lesson we're going to take advantage of a function called the ALL function. And what it is going to do is allow us to override the filter context for that particular expression. And where these are really useful is when you're creating ratio calculations. Because as you can imagine, if you had no way of overriding the filter context, your numerator and denominator would always be the same because they would always be equally affected by the filter context. Okay, so let's look at an application of this function. So what I'm going to do is go to my Sales table, right click, say new measure. Go ahead into the formula bar here, drop my line of code. So it says, for this particular expression here, go ahead and ignore all the filters. So remove all the filters that are affecting the Sales table for this expression. Then we go ahead and apply the COUNTROWS. So if I go like that, hit enter. Let's go ahead and format this before we go any further. So put the thousands separator in here. And that is because the filter has been removed.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-all-function?u=76281980&t=104)** So when we take a look at this row right here, we are going through and doing account of the Sales Orders Line Items, but it's ignoring this value of 2020. > [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/disable-the-auto-date-time-setting?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so in this sub lesson we're going to disable the auto date-time setting, which we had insured was set back at the very beginning of the course. Okay, so what I want to do here first though, is explain why we're going to do this in the first place. So if I go and take a look at my customer table, I'm going to see that I've got a column here called birth date. And given that I've got the auto date-time settings turned on, this column here, which is set as a date, will automatically get a hierarchy built on it whether I like it or not. So birth date has been declared as a date data type. And therefore probably automatically build this thing called a date hierarchy that goes year, quarter, month, date. And it will do that for every column that it sees in your data model, this is of the date data type. So same thing here with my order date. I've got the order date and order date table. It builds the hierarchy called date hierarchy with the levels in it, I get no control over on it. So [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] is in some ways just trying to be friendly and be helpful to you. But in some ways I find this a very- A feature you should just simply turn off. You should own the control over what date columns actually get hierarchies built on. So this is one where I like full control over the development. And the reason why that's important is we see a lot of Power BI models where people are pulling data from various sources and there's a lot of columns that have date information in them. And if that's the case,
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/disable-the-auto-date-time-setting?u=76281980&t=98)** your model will have a date hierarchy built for every one of the columns in your model, whether you like it or not. And that can really overinflate the size of your model. I worked with one customer a couple years ago when this feature was first enabled for the ability to turn that off. And what ended up happening is they did not realize that was a feature they could turn off. They had a number of Power BI models that were built up. After I kind of walked through the hows and the whys behind this. They said, "Oh, is that really the case?" And they went away and a few days later they came back and said, "You know, I'm glad you gave us this information because we cut the size of our models down by 80%." 80% savings just by clicking this checkbox, I'm going to show you how to turn off here in a moment. And that is just because Power BI is once again trying to be friendly and helpful, but in my opinion, it does not do a good job of this. You own, control the hierarchies, you set them up the way you want, wherever you want. The nice thing about that is you can name the hierarchy what you want. You can build the levels the way you want, and you get to decide how things are built up. So I just want to point out here that the birthdate has a hierarchy on it automatically. And order date has one as well. So what I'm going to do here to turn this off, I'm going to go to file, options and settings, options. >
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/disable-the-auto-date-time-setting?u=76281980&t=190)** I come back into my Power BI model and now those hierarchies are gone. Now my model had two, not that big a deal, but if your model had 10, 15, 20, or 30 different date columns, which is not unusual in analytic models, you would have a bunch of hierarchies whether you liked them or not. And the worst part about these hierarchies that were being built up for this customer of ours is they weren't even being used and they were very large 'cause they had birth dates that went all the way back to the early 1900s. So had these hierarchies that weren't important, weren't being used, and were consuming an inordinate amount of space in the data models. Okay, so there we go. We've turned that setting off. And right now there's only one, it's order date. So, I'm going to click on that. And we're going to notice it says it was validated successfully. So, you may ask yourself, well, what validation was it doing? So, what I'm going to do here is we can just go ahead and click this link right here, which I'm going to go ahead and do which brings us to this article from [[Microsoft]] which dives a little deeper into setting up and using date tables in the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] desktop. So, what I'm going to do is just scroll down a little bit, and show you the way that the validation is occurring. So, what Power BI is doing is taking a look at these four bullet points right here. It's making sure that the row values for order date contain unique values. So, that means every date can show up only once. And if you recall back in [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]], that's the way we want our dimension to be set up. We want uniqueness. It's going to check to make sure there's no null values in any of the rows for that particular column.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/designate-a-date-table?u=76281980&t=100)** We don't have any because keep in mind what we did right off the bat to create the table was we used the calendar function inside of [[DAX]] to say build me a table that has all the dates from the earliest date in the sales table to the latest date. And then the next one here is related to the point that is made. It wants to see contiguous date values from beginning to end. So, beginning to end means the start of the data in your fact table to the end of the data in your fact table, which we essentially got by using the calendar function. The last bullet point we can largely ignore if you follow my best practice, and do not use the date time data type, but just for completeness sake, it's basically saying if you use the date time data type, you want to make sure it has the same timestamp across each one of the values in the rows. But given that I avoid the date time data type, only these first three matter. Okay, so I'm back in my Power BI desktop. I've explained the validation and the fact that our table is valid. I'm now going to click OK. And what we're going to see here now is that this table has been marked as a date table. It has this little card with a person on it, which means now that this is our date table, and this is the column inside of our date table, which we'll be using as a reference. So, in the next few sub-lessons here, we're going to be referencing this date table as we start building time intelligence functions up. Okay, with that, we're at the end of the sub-lesson.

#### [Explore the structure of a .pbix file](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/explore-the-structure-of-a-pbix-file?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/explore-the-structure-of-a-pbix-file?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] All right so throughout this course we have been building up a file called Sales.pbix and just wanted to briefly show you what the contents of that file are. And as I've mentioned earlier on in the course here,is that one of the secret sauces in behind PowerBI is the fact that the pbix file is actually broken into two different pieces. So we can see here in this diagram that we've got our dataset and our report and the ability to keep the data and the report as two separate objects is in my mind one of the very important architectural differentiations between [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and PowerBI. Because Excel bundles your data and reporting all into one, it's very difficult to separate the two out and it's very difficult to achieve any level of sharing. Whereas with PowerBI, the fact that we've got a separate data set, which is often where a good portion of your work goes in any analytic endeavor, we can go ahead and put all that work into the dataset, make it usable by many different users, and then ultimately share that dataset so we can avoid repeating that work over and over and over again, which is a systemic problem in most Excel spreadsheets where users find themselves building spreadsheets that are very similar to other spreadsheets have that are similar to other ones they have, they build more that are similar to the ones they have and you end up with a real [[Data Management]] mess because you have so many spreadsheets that are similar to each other. And the real problem there is the fact that it's difficult to manage and it's a big waste of time. Whereas in PowerBI we can focus on creating very good
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/explore-the-structure-of-a-pbix-file?u=76281980&t=100)** and reusable data sets and then build reporting on top of it. So some of the structures I want to talk about on the reporting side are that you on the PowerBI desktop pbix file have a single report. So as we build the sales.pbix file, we end up with a single report that'll end up with the name of the pbix file and it'll be called sales. And to wrap the discussion up in the dataset, when we eventually publish this into the PowerBI service, we end up with a dataset also called sales. Within that report we have, we have the ability to create one or many pages and then each page can contain zero or many visuals. So this is an important set of relationships to understand in terms of how the dataset works, how the report works, when the published process is invoked, which we'll talk about in the next lesson, is it will actually break this into its constituent parts. And then once we get in the service, we'll actually see a data set called sales and a report called sales. And then from that point on we can go ahead and start building more on top of these data sets. Alright, so with that, we're at the end of the sub lesson.

> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)

#### [Explore the Power BI service](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/explore-the-power-bi-service?u=76281980)
