#### [Set an alert](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/set-an-alert?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/set-an-alert?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] All right, so we are back over on our dashboard and we're going to look to set an alert. So we set alerts in the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] service to notify you when data on a dashboard changes above or below limits you set. Alerts can be set on tiles pinned from report visuals or from Power BI Q&A, but only on gauges, KPIs, and cards. So that is one of the limitations on alerts is the fact that you can do them on a gauge visualization, a KPI visual, or a card visualization. And then alerts only work on data that's refreshed. When data refreshes, Power BI looks to see if an alert is set for that data. If the data has reached an alert threshold, an alert is triggered. And alerts can be created in the Power BI service by customers that have any license type. And we can set alerts on dashboard tiles that you have created and saved in My Workspace that have been shared with you in premium reserve capacity or in any workspace you can access. And then the question is who can see the alerts you create? And only you can see alerts you set even if you share your dashboard. Data alerts are fully synchronized across all the platforms and we set and view data alerts in the Power BI service, but once again, only you can go ahead and see them once they're created. All right, so that sounds like an awful lot of limitations around alerts. They're still very useful for you as an individual. So let's go ahead and show you how to go ahead and create one. So I'm going to go ahead and on the KPI card here, 'cause this is the only visual which I have, which I can actually go ahead and create an alert on.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/set-an-alert?u=76281980&t=100)** Click on this. I'm going to say manage alerts and then the manage alert dialog box is going to come up. I'm going to click on the Add Alert Rule, I'm going to make sure it's on and we'll leave the name called Total Sales. And what I'm doing here is I'm going to set an alert rule for total sales and what I'll do here is I'm going to say whenever the sales goes above $7 million and at most every 24 hours we are going to do an alert and alerts, once again, are only sent if your data changes. And by default, you'll receive notifications on the service in the notification center and actually want this to send me the email as well. So I'm going to go ahead and get this alert set as is and say Save and Close. So now I have an alert set on this tile. If I want to go back in and take a look at it, I can click on this, go to manage alerts and now I'll see that I've got this alert called Total Sales, which I can click on and go back here and configure. All right, so alerts certainly have their use, they are something you'll find yourself using as an individual, but that is one of the limitations of it is you can't go ahead and build alert that you intend to share to other people because the alert is really meant for you. If you do want to go build alerts that go beyond Power BI or extend the functionality, you can go into Manage Alerts here. There's a link right at the very bottom that says use Power Automate to trigger additional actions. And if I was to go ahead and click on this, this will take me over to the help page on how you can go ahead and use Power Automate
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/set-an-alert?u=76281980&t=194)** to make things more efficient over inside of Power BI that really kind of go beyond basic alerting over inside of the Power BI service. And we can see here now that it's come up, it's basically allowing me to go ahead if I have the skills in Power Automate, which are beyond the scope of this course here, to go in and trigger a flow with Power BI data and allow a more sophisticated alerting process and [[Workflow Management]] to kickoff. All right, so just back over to our dashboard tile here. This is the alert we went in through and took a look at exposing it to Power Automate if you want to do things that are more sophisticated, but that's all we really need to show you here as a level one user. And with that, we're at the end of the sublesson.

#### [Use the Q&A dialog](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-q-a-dialog?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-q-a-dialog?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so let's go ahead and demo using Q and A. This is one of the features I find the most intriguing inside of [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]], and it is because it allows you to go ahead and ask English like questions of your data and have Power BI return results. Now, one thing I kind of like to say about this Q and A functionality is it is really much better supported if you use good naming conventions. And if you recall back earlier in the course when we were building our tables and call names, I really tried to emphasize the importance of good naming conventions. And this is kind of where we really get to see a lot of the benefit of those naming conventions way back in our data model. So to use Q and A, I'm just going to go ahead and at the top of my dashboard, 'cause I'm on the dashboard page here, I can click this, ask a question about your data and that's just going to go ahead and pop up. And now I can actually type a question in. So I'm going to type in total sales like so. And when I go ahead and type my first few words in, Power BI is going to take what I typed and say, all right, well based on the question you've asked so far, this is the best visual. So it just takes a card and gives you our 26.09 million. And I may want to say by product name, so I'm going to say by product name and give Power BI a second here. And now it's going to say, well now that you've asked the question that way perhaps this is the best visual for going ahead and displaying information. And then you do have the ability to control what visuals.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-q-a-dialog?u=76281980&t=101)** So I can say on a pie chart, which is going to look terrible, but just to show you that you can go ahead and do it. So there we go, we've got our measure total sales by product name on a pie chart, and we typed this all out in an English like sentence and really borrowed from the table and naming convention on our columns and measures back down the data model. So here's where we get to see the power of really nice naming conventions in our data model. So is as much, you know, I have to admit, building naming conventions and coming up with them and getting people to agree it is not the most fun topic in the world 'cause there's a lot of contention around that topic. But once you get there, what you can do with it is absolutely incredible. But the hardest part of the whole thing is actually getting the conventions agreed to in the first place. So I'm just going to go ahead and let's get rid of that pie chart 'cause nobody wants to see this on a pie chart. Get us back over to here. At this point in time I can go ahead and pin my visual and I could even go in and start doing some editing of the visual so I could go in here and put some new data on it, go in and configure things. So I do have the ability to, you know, work with this, like I built my reports and their tiles back down to the Power BI desktop. I can configure things from this point. So if I pin this, this would put it in the dashboard and if I do this work here, it would then go ahead and save this work into my tile here and allow me to do any, some further editing. So once again, much much like you would do back down the Power BI desktop itself.

> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/use-the-q-a-dialog?u=76281980&t=194)** Alright, so that is it for this sub lesson. Like I say, I'm really excited about Q and A and this is part of the class that I really emphasize a lot and just in terms of the value of those naming expansions way back when we were slogging our way through the data model. ### 11. Implementing Security and Access

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives-23455255?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/learning-objectives-23455255?u=76281980&t=6)** - In this sub lesson, we're going to learn how to implement security and access controls. To do that in lesson 11.1, we're going to create roll level security roles. Then in 11.2, we're going to validate those roll level security roles using the [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] desktop. And finally, we're going to assign and validate those roles in the Power BI service. Let us begin.

#### [Create row-level security (RLS) roles](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-row-level-security-rls-roles?u=76281980)

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-row-level-security-rls-roles?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Okay, so we are back down on our [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] Desktop, and this is where we need to go and create our role-level security initially and get the rules configured. So let's go ahead and get our roles set up first. So I'm just going to go ahead to the Modeling tab, click on Manage roles, and now I get my managed security rules dialogue box come popping up. And the first thing I want to do is I'm going to go through and create a new role by clicking New and just double clicking here. I'm going to say North America Controller. So that's the very first role we're going to create. So let's go ahead and define that role. So I'm going to go to Sales Territory, and I click on Add. And what we're going to say here is Any, so whenever any of these rules are true, I want to go through and say Sales Territory Country, if it equals Canada. Let's go and add another rule, Sales Territory Country if it equals United States, like so. So there's our first rule. So if somebody is a member of the North America Controller group, they will be able to go in and see data for Canada and United States only. So if I want to look at this in [[DAX]] I can switch this switch to this DAX Editor, and here's the code that sits behind the scene. It says, "Whenever a Sales Territory Country equals Canada, or Sales Territory Country equals the United States, that user will go ahead and see those values." So I just close the box out. I'm going to get back into my managed security roles,
>

So there's our very first one that we've created. So I could go ahead and create another one. So let's go and create Australia this time. So I'm going to go through and set my Australia controller up. So same thing, I'm going to go New, and I come in here, see Australia Controller, [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]], and I'm going to make sure I'm on Sales Territories. I'm going to say Add. I'm going to say when Any equals Australia, like that. There we go, so now we've got that rule set up, and if I want, I can go through and duplicate this, so I can go like this and say duplicate, and just to show that we got some of it all already created here. So I could go in here and say Europe Controller, something like that. And then I could come over here and start editing this one as well. So in this case, I would edit this and say France. And I'm going to choose that and say Any, say Sales Territory Country again, and say Germany, like so. And finally, we'll go ahead and add the last one in here. And the last country we're going to add in is the United Kingdom. So let's go ahead and drop that in and say Sales Territory Country equals United Kingdom. And go ahead and Save. So I just want to go back and quickly review my rule from Australia Controller, Sales Territory Country equals Australia, Europe Controller Sales Territory Country equals France,
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/create-row-level-security-rls-roles?u=76281980&t=195)** Germany, or United Kingdom. That's Any. And same thing with United North American Controller, Sales Territory Country equals Canada or United States. So I'm going to close this now that I verified that has all been saved off. And in the next section, we're going to go ahead and validate these rollover security rules. So with that, we're at the end of this sub-lesson.

So now that I've got that set, I'm going to go ahead and publish it. It'll ask me to save. So I'm kind of glad that mistake happened because that is a lesson to be learned 'cause we will all do that, accidentally have the report set in a different state and when we publish it, that becomes the default state. So I'm going to click select there and it's going to let me know that what I'm doing here with this data set may impact two reports and one dashboard. So I can view the impact if I want to. And then build very few reports on top of that and then put security on top of those reports so that many people can get out of them and you can restrict them to only see the data that you want them to see. So an incredible amount of power inside of Power BI, this is all role of a security. When you start advancing your skills inside of Power BI, you will eventually bump into something called Dynamic Role of Security, which is beyond scope for this particular class, but that's the next level of security inside of Power BI from a data perspective. ### 12. Summary

#### [Summary](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/summary?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-power-bi-for-data-analysts-by-microsoft-press/summary?u=76281980&t=6)** - All right, so we've covered an awful lot of ground over these few lessons, and hopefully you feel more confident now in taking [[Microsoft Power BI|Power BI]] and using it more within your business on a daily basis. So let's just recap what we covered. In lesson one, we talked about [[Getting Started With Power Bi]]. In lesson two, we import and transform data using the [[Microsoft Power Query|Power Query]] Editor. In lesson three, we enhanced the data model with some basic functionality. In lesson four, we enhanced the data model and got into [[DAX]] Basics. In lesson five, we explored DAX Time Intelligence. In lesson six, we introduced you to quick measures. In lesson number seven, we started building desktop visuals and talked about the basics of that first. In lesson eight, we went in and took things to the next level. In lesson nine, we used the Power BI Service reading view. In lesson 10, we extended the Power BI Service and took things to the next level. And finally, in lesson number 11, we implemented security and access. Hopefully, you found this very entertaining, very informative, and mostly you now have the confidence to go out and build solutions for your business and show people just what Power BI can do. Thank you very much, and enjoy.
